Hydrogen Bonding: Many hosts (such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers) use hydrogen bonds to selectively bind guests, including small organic molecules, ions, or biomolecules.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., functionalized macrocycles or metal-organic frameworks) can interact with oppositely charged guests, such as metal cations or anionic species.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (like cucurbiturils or pillararenes) can engage in π-π stacking with aromatic guest molecules, which is particularly useful for sensing polyaromatic hydrocarbons or dyes.\n4. Upon introduction of the target analyte (guest), the analyte competes with the indicator for binding to the host. Displacement of the indicator alters the electrochemical signal (such as current or potential), providing a quantitative readout. The most common types of host-guest interactions include:\n\n1. Hydrogen Bonding: Many hosts (such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers) use hydrogen bonds to selectively bind guests, including small organic molecules, ions, or biomolecules.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., functionalized macrocycles or metal-organic frameworks) can interact with oppositely charged guests, such as metal cations or anionic species.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (like cucurbiturils or pillararenes) can engage in π-π stacking with aromatic guest molecules, which is particularly useful for sensing polyaromatic hydrocarbons or dyes.\n4. Hydrophobic Interactions: Nonpolar guests can be encapsulated by hydrophobic cavities of hosts (e.g., cyclodextrins), leading to displacement of the indicator and a change in electrochemical response.\n5. Upon introduction of the target analyte (guest), the analyte competes with the indicator for binding to the host. Hydrogen Bonding: Many hosts (such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers) use hydrogen bonds to selectively bind guests, including small organic molecules, ions, or biomolecules.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., functionalized macrocycles or metal-organic frameworks) can interact with oppositely charged guests, such as metal cations or anionic species.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (like cucurbiturils or pillararenes) can engage in π-π stacking with aromatic guest molecules, which is particularly useful for sensing polyaromatic hydrocarbons or dyes.\n4. Upon introduction of the target analyte (guest), the analyte competes with the indicator for binding to the host. Displacement of the indicator alters the electrochemical signal (such as current or potential), providing a quantitative readout. Upon introduction of the target analyte (guest), the analyte competes with the indicator for binding to the host.
